<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_Hinduism_terms" title="Glossary of Hinduism terms">Glossary of Hinduism terms</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/File:Aum_Om_red.svg" class="image"><img alt="Aum Om red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Aum_Om_red.svg/16px-Aum_Om_red.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Aum_Om_red.svg/23px-Aum_Om_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Aum_Om_red.svg/31px-Aum_Om_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="356" data-file-height="367" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Hinduism" title="Portal:Hinduism">Hinduism&#32;portal</a></i> <a href="/wiki/File:HinduSwastika.svg" class="image"><img alt="HinduSwastika.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/HinduSwastika.svg/15px-HinduSwastika.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/HinduSwastika.svg/23px-HinduSwastika.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/HinduSwastika.svg/30px-HinduSwastika.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="142" data-file-height="145" /></a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Hinduism" title="Template:Hinduism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hinduism" title="Template talk:Hinduism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Hinduism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Holi</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small> </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span>); is a popular ancient <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> festival, originating from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. It is celebrated predominantly in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, but has also spread to other areas of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> through the diaspora from the Indian subcontinent. Holi is popularly known as the Indian <b>"festival of spring"</b>, the <b>"festival of colours"</b>, or the <b>"festival of love"</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-yg_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yg-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OED-Holi_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Holi-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The festival signifies the arrival of spring, the end of winter, the blossoming of love, and for many a festive day to meet others, play and laugh, forget and forgive, and repair broken relationships.<sup id="cite_ref-keholi_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keholi-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wd_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wd-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The festival also celebrates the beginning of a good spring harvest season.<sup id="cite_ref-keholi_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keholi-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wd_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wd-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It lasts for a night and a day, starting on the evening of the <a href="/wiki/Purnima" title="Purnima">Purnima</a> (Full Moon day) falling in the <a href="/wiki/Vikram_Samvat" title="Vikram Samvat">Vikram Samvat Calendar</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> in the Hindu calendar month of <a href="/wiki/Phalgun" class="mw-redirect" title="Phalgun">Phalgun</a>, which falls around middle of March in the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>. The first evening is known as <b>Holika Dahan</b> (burning of demon holika) or <b>Chhoti Holi</b> and the following day as Holi, <b>Rangwali Holi</b>, <b>Dhuleti</b>, <b>Dhulandi</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> or <b>Phagwah</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holi is an ancient <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> religious festival which has become popular with non-Hindus as well in many parts of South Asia, as well as people of other communities outside Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-keholi_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keholi-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In addition to India and Nepal, the festival is celebrated by Indian subcontinent diaspora in countries such as Jamaica,<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson2004_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2004-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Suriname, Guyana, Trinidad and Tobago, South Africa, Malaysia, the United Kingdom, the United States, Canada, Mauritius, and Fiji.<sup id="cite_ref-yg_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yg-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-online.wsj.com_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-online.wsj.com-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In recent years the festival has spread to parts of Europe and North America as a spring celebration of love, frolic, and colours.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-online.wsj.com_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-online.wsj.com-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holi_Festival_of_Colours_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holi_Festival_of_Colours-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holi celebrations start on the night before Holi with a <a href="/wiki/Holika_Dahan" title="Holika Dahan">Holika Dahan</a> where people gather, perform religious rituals in front of the bonfire, and pray that their internal evil be destroyed the way <a href="/wiki/Holika" title="Holika">Holika</a>, the sister of the demon king <a href="/wiki/Hiranyakashipu" title="Hiranyakashipu">Hiranyakashipu</a>, was killed in the fire. The next morning is celebrated as Rangwali Holi – a free-for-all festival of colours,<sup id="cite_ref-keholi_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keholi-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> where people smear each other with colours and drench each other. Water guns and water-filled balloons are also used to play and colour each other. Anyone and everyone is fair game, friend or stranger, rich or poor, man or woman, children, and elders. The frolic and fight with colours occurs in the open streets, open parks, outside temples and buildings. Groups carry drums and other musical instruments, go from place to place, sing and dance. People visit family, friends and foes to throw coloured powders on each other, laugh and gossip, then share Holi delicacies, food and drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-cj_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cj-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-williamsholifood_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamsholifood-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Some customary drinks include <a href="/wiki/Bhang" title="Bhang">bhang</a> (made from <a href="/wiki/Cannabis" title="Cannabis">cannabis</a>), which is intoxicating.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TOIBhang_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TOIBhang-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In the evening, after sobering up, people dress up and visit friends and family.<sup id="cite_ref-ht_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ht-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cj_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cj-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<p><span id="History_and_Significance"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance">Significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Holika" title="Holika">Holika</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg/170px-Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg/255px-Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg/340px-Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_Bonfire_Udaipur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Holika bonfire in front of Jagdish Temple in <a href="/wiki/Udaipur" title="Udaipur">Udaipur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, 2010</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vishnu_legend">Vishnu legend</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Vishnu legend">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is a symbolic legend to explain why Holi is celebrated as a festival of triumph of good over evil in the honour of Hindu god <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> and his follower <a href="/wiki/Prahlada" title="Prahlada">Prahlada</a>. King <a href="/wiki/Hiranyakashipu" title="Hiranyakashipu">Hiranyakashipu</a>, according to a legend found in chapter 7 of <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> was the king of demonic <a href="/wiki/Asura" title="Asura">Asuras</a>, and had earned a <a href="/wiki/Blessing" title="Blessing">boon</a> that gave him five special powers: he could be killed by neither a human being nor an animal, neither indoors nor outdoors, neither at day nor at night, neither by <i>astra</i> (projectile weapons) nor by any <i>shastra</i> (handheld weapons), and neither on land nor in water or air. Hiranyakashipu grew arrogant, thought he was God, and demanded that everyone worship only him.<sup id="cite_ref-ht_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ht-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hiranyakashipu's own son, <a href="/wiki/Prahlada" title="Prahlada">Prahlada</a>, however, disagreed. He was and remained devoted to Vishnu.<sup id="cite_ref-cj_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cj-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> This infuriated Hiranyakashipu. He subjected Prahlada to cruel punishments, none of which affected the boy or his resolve to do what he thought was right. Finally, Holika, Prahlada's evil aunt, tricked him into sitting on a pyre with her.<sup id="cite_ref-ht_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ht-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Holika was wearing a <a href="/wiki/Cloak" title="Cloak">cloak</a> that made her immune to injury from fire, while Prahlada was not. As the fire roared, the cloak flew from Holika and encased Prahlada,<sup id="cite_ref-cj_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cj-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> who survived while Holika burned. Vishnu, the god who appears as an <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatar</a> to restore <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a> in Hindu beliefs, took the form of <a href="/wiki/Narasimha" title="Narasimha">Narasimha</a> - half human and half lion (which is neither a human nor an animal), at dusk (when it was neither day nor night), took Hiranyakashyapu at a doorstep (which was neither indoors nor outdoors), placed him on his lap (which was neither land, water nor air), and then eviscerated and killed the king with his lion claws (which were neither a handheld weapon nor a launched weapon).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Holika bonfire and Holi signifies the celebration of the symbolic victory of good over evil, of Prahlada over Hiranyakashipu, and of the fire that burned Holika.<sup id="cite_ref-wd_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wd-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Importance_of_the_Festival"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Krishna_legend">Krishna legend</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Krishna legend">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Braj" title="Braj">Braj</a> region of India, where the Hindu deity <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> grew up, the festival is celebrated until <a href="/wiki/Rang_Panchami" title="Rang Panchami">Rang Panchmi</a> in commemoration of the divine love of <a href="/wiki/Radha" title="Radha">Radha</a> for Krishna. The festivities officially usher in spring, with Holi celebrated as a festival of love.<sup id="cite_ref-ind_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ind-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> There is a symbolic myth behind commemorating Krishna as well. As a baby, Krishna developed his characteristic dark skin colour because the she-demon <i><a href="/wiki/Putana" title="Putana">Putana</a></i> poisoned him with her breast milk.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>  In his youth, Krishna despaired whether the fair-skinned Radha would like him because of his dark skin colour. His mother, tired of his desperation, asks him to approach Radha and ask her to colour his face in any colour she wanted. This she did, and Radha and Krishna became a couple. Ever since, the playful colouring of Radha and Krishna's face has been commemorated as Holi.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Beyond India, these legends help to explain the significance of Holi (<i>Phagwah</i>) are common in some Caribbean and South American communities of Indian origin such as <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> It is also celebrated with great fervour in Mauritius.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kama_and_Rati_legend">Kama and Rati legend</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Kama and Rati legend">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Among other Hindu traditions such as <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shaktism</a>, the legendary significance of Holi is linked to <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">yoga</a> and deep meditation, goddess <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Parvati</a> wanting to bring back Shiva into the world, seeks help from the Hindu god of love called <a href="/wiki/Kamadeva" title="Kamadeva">Kamadeva</a> on <a href="/wiki/Vasant_Panchami" title="Vasant Panchami">Vasant Panchami</a>. The love god shoots arrows at Shiva, the yogi opens his third eye and burns Kama to ashes. This upsets both Kama's wife <a href="/wiki/Rati" title="Rati">Rati</a> (<i>Kamadevi</i>) and his own wife <i>Parvati</i>. <i>Rati</i> performs her own meditative asceticism for forty days, upon which Shiva understands, forgives out of compassion and restores the god of love. This return of the god of love, is celebrated on the 40th day after Vasant Panchami festival as Holi.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The Kama legend and its significance to Holi has many variant forms, particularly in South India.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cultural significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Holi festival has a cultural significance among various Hindu traditions of the Indian subcontinent. It is the festive day to end and rid oneself of past errors, to end conflicts by meeting others, a day to forget and forgive. People pay or forgive debts, as well as deal anew with those in their lives. Holi also marks the start of spring, for many the start of the new year, an occasion for people to enjoy the changing seasons and make new friends.<sup id="cite_ref-wd_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wd-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ihh_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihh-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Indian_religions">Other Indian religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other Indian religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucknow,_Uttar_Pradesh,_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Lucknow%2C_Uttar_Pradesh%2C_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Lucknow%2C_Uttar_Pradesh%2C_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Lucknow%2C_Uttar_Pradesh%2C_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Lucknow%2C_Uttar_Pradesh%2C_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Lucknow%2C_Uttar_Pradesh%2C_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Lucknow%2C_Uttar_Pradesh%2C_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3369" data-file-height="4156" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucknow,_Uttar_Pradesh,_India_-_The_emperor_Jahangir_celebrating_the_Festival_of_Holi_with_the_ladies_of_the_zenana_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mughal_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal India">Mughal Indian</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> celebrating Holi with ladies of the <a href="/wiki/Zenana" title="Zenana">zenana</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The festival has traditionally been also observed by non-Hindus, such as by Jains<sup id="cite_ref-wiley42_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiley42-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and Newar Buddhists (<a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-bal269_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bal269-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Mughal_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal India">Mughal India</a>, Holi was celebrated with such exuberance that people of all castes could throw colour on the Emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-Safvi2016_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Safvi2016-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> According to Sharma (2017), "there are several paintings of Mughal emperors celebrating Holi".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  Grand celebrations of Holi were held at the <a href="/wiki/Lal_Qila_(Delhi)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lal Qila (Delhi)">Lal Qila</a>, where the festival was also known as <i>Eid-e-gulaabi</i> or <i>Aab-e-Pashi</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Safvi2016_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Safvi2016-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mehfil" title="Mehfil">Mehfils</a> were held throughout the walled city of Delhi with aristocrats and traders alike participating.<sup id="cite_ref-Safvi2016_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Safvi2016-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_Zafar" title="Bahadur Shah Zafar">Bahadur Shah Zafar</a> himself wrote a song for the festival, while poets such as <a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrau" class="mw-redirect" title="Amir Khusrau">Amir Khusrau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raskhan" title="Raskhan">Ibrahim Raskhan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazeer_Akbarabadi" title="Nazeer Akbarabadi">Nazeer Akbarabadi</a> and Mehjoor Lakhnavi relished it in their writings.<sup id="cite_ref-Safvi2016_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Safvi2016-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhs have traditionally celebrated the festival, at least through the 19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p95_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p95-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> with its historic texts referring to it as <i>Hola</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Roy2005p192_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy2005p192-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> – the last human guru of the Sikhs – modified Holi with a three-day <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> extension festival of martial arts. The extension started the day after the Holi festival in <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur Sahib</a>, where Sikh soldiers would train in mock battles, compete in horsemanship, athletics, archery and military exercises.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holi was observed by <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Maharaja Ranjit Singh</a> and his Sikh Empire that extended across what are now northern parts of India and Pakistan. According to a report by Tribune India, Sikh court records state that 300 mounds of colours were used in 1837 by Ranjit Singh and his officials in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>. Ranjit Singh would celebrate Holi with others in the Bilawal gardens, where decorative tents were set up. In 1837, Sir Henry Fane who was the commander-in-chief of the British Indian army joined the Holi celebrations organised by Ranjit Singh. A mural in the Lahore Fort was sponsored by Ranjit Singh and it showed the Hindu god Krishna playing Holi with gopis. After the death of Ranjit Singh, his Sikh sons and others continued to play Holi every year with colours and lavish festivities. The colonial British officials joined these celebrations.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radha_celebrating_Holi,_c1788.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Radha_celebrating_Holi%2C_c1788.jpg/220px-Radha_celebrating_Holi%2C_c1788.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Radha_celebrating_Holi%2C_c1788.jpg/330px-Radha_celebrating_Holi%2C_c1788.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Radha_celebrating_Holi%2C_c1788.jpg/440px-Radha_celebrating_Holi%2C_c1788.jpg 2x" data-file-width="767" data-file-height="482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radha_celebrating_Holi,_c1788.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Radha" title="Radha">Radha</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gopi" title="Gopi">Gopis</a> celebrating Holi, with accompaniment of music instruments</div></div></div>
<p>Holi is an important spring festival for Hindus, a national holiday in India and Nepal with regional holidays in other countries. To many Hindus and some non-Hindus, it is a playful cultural event and an excuse to throw coloured water at friends or strangers in jest. It is also observed broadly in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Holi is celebrated at the end of winter, on the last <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">full moon</a> day of the Hindu luni-solar calendar month marking the spring, making the date vary with the lunar cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-holicalendars_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holicalendars-6">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> The date falls typically in March, but sometimes late February of the Gregorian calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The festival has many purposes; most prominently, it celebrates the beginning of Spring. In 17th century literature, it was identified as a festival that celebrated agriculture, commemorated good spring harvests and the fertile land.<sup id="cite_ref-keholi_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keholi-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Hindus believe it is a time of enjoying spring's abundant colours and saying farewell to winter. To many Hindus, Holi festivities mark the beginning of the new year as well as an occasion to reset and renew ruptured relationships, end conflicts and rid themselves of accumulated emotional impurities from the past.<sup id="cite_ref-wd_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wd-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ihh_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihh-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It also has a religious purpose, symbolically signified by the legend of Holika. The night before Holi, bonfires are lit in a ceremony known as Holika Dahan (burning of <a href="/wiki/Holika" title="Holika">Holika</a>) or Little Holi. People gather near fires, sing and dance. The next day, Holi, also known as <i>Dhuli</i> in Sanskrit, or <i>Dhulheti</i>, <i>Dhulandi</i> or <i>Dhulendi</i>, is celebrated.
</p><p>In Northern parts of India, Children and youth spray coloured powder solutions (<i>gulal</i>) at each other, laugh and celebrate, while adults smear dry coloured powder (<i>abir</i>) on each other's faces.<sup id="cite_ref-ht_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ht-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ihh_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihh-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Visitors to homes are first teased with colours, then served with Holi delicacies (such as <i>puranpoli</i>, <i>dahi-bada</i> and <i>gujia</i>), desserts and drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-williamsholifood_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williamsholifood-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> After playing with colours, and cleaning up, people bathe, put on clean clothes, and visit friends and family.<sup id="cite_ref-wd_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wd-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Rangapanchami"></span> Like Holika Dahan, <i>Kama Dahanam</i> is celebrated in some parts of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. The festival of colours in these parts is called <i>Rangapanchami</i>, and occurs on the fifth day after Poornima (full moon).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_rituals">History and rituals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History and rituals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Holi festival is an ancient Hindu festival with its cultural rituals. It is mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, Dasakumara Charita, and by the poet <a href="/wiki/K%C4%81lid%C4%81sa" title="Kālidāsa">Kālidāsa</a> during the 4th century reign of Chandragupta II.<sup id="cite_ref-yg_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yg-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The celebration of Holi is also mentioned in the 7th-century Sanskrit drama <i><a href="/wiki/Ratnavali" title="Ratnavali">Ratnavali</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ori_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ori-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  The festival of Holi caught the fascination of European traders and British colonial staff by the 17th century. Various old editions of Oxford English Dictionary mention it, but with varying, phonetically derived spellings: <i>Houly</i> (1687), <i>Hooly</i> (1698), <i>Huli</i> (1789), <i>Hohlee</i> (1809), <i>Hoolee</i> (1825), and <i>Holi</i> in editions published after 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-keholi_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keholi-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are several cultural rituals associated with Holi:<sup id="cite_ref-htor_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-htor-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holika_Dahan">Holika Dahan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Holika Dahan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Holika_Dahan" title="Holika Dahan">Holika Dahan</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation">Preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Days before the festival people start gathering wood and combustible materials for the bonfire in parks, community centers, near temples and other open spaces. On top of the pyre is an effigy to signify Holika who tricked Prahalad into the fire. Inside homes, people stock up on pigments, food, party drinks and festive seasonal foods such as <i><a href="/wiki/Gujiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Gujiya">gujiya</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mathri" title="Mathri">mathri</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Malpua" title="Malpua">malpuas</a></i> and other regional delicacies.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bonfire">Bonfire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bonfire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On the eve of Holi, typically at or after sunset, the pyre is lit, signifying Holika Dahan. The ritual symbolises the victory of good over evil. People gather around the fire to sing and dance.<sup id="cite_ref-wd_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wd-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Play">Play</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Play">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Playing_with_colours">Playing with colours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Playing with colours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In North and Western India, Holi frolic and celebrations begin the morning after the Holika bonfire. There is no tradition of holding <i>puja</i> (prayer), and the day is for partying and pure enjoyment. Children and young people form groups armed with dry colours, coloured solution and <a href="/wiki/Water_guns" class="mw-redirect" title="Water guns">water guns</a> (<i>pichkaris</i>), water balloons filled with coloured water, and other creative means to colour their targets.<sup id="cite_ref-htor_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-htor-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, washable natural plant-derived colours such as <a href="/wiki/Turmeric" title="Turmeric">turmeric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neem" class="mw-redirect" title="Neem">neem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butea_monosperma" title="Butea monosperma">dhak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kumkum" class="mw-redirect" title="Kumkum">kumkum</a> were used, but water-based commercial pigments are increasingly used. All colours are used. Everyone in open areas such as streets and parks is game, but inside homes or at doorways only dry powder is used to smear each other's face. People throw colours and get their targets completely coloured up. It is like a <a href="/wiki/Water_fight" title="Water fight">water fight</a>, but with coloured water. People take delight in spraying coloured water on each other. By late morning, everyone looks like a canvas of colours. This is why Holi is given the name "Festival of Colours".
</p><p>Groups sing and dance, some playing drums and <i><a href="/wiki/Dholak" title="Dholak">dholak</a></i>. After each stop of fun and play with colours, people offer <a href="/wiki/Gujiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Gujiya">gujiya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathri" title="Mathri">mathri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malpua" title="Malpua">malpuas</a> and other traditional delicacies.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Cold drinks, including adult drinks based on local intoxicating herbs,<sup id="cite_ref-TOIBhang_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TOIBhang-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> are also part of the Holi festivity.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_variations">Other variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Braj" title="Braj">Braj</a> region around <a href="/wiki/Mathura" title="Mathura">Mathura</a>, in north India, the festivities may last more than a week. The rituals go beyond playing with colours, and include a day where men go around with shields and women have the right to playfully beat them on their shields with sticks.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In south India, some worship and make offerings to Kaamadeva, the love god of Indian mythology.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_after_party">The after party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: The after party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After a day of play with colours, people clean up, wash and bathe, sober up and dress up in the evening and greet friends and relatives by visiting them and exchanging sweets. Holi is also a festival of forgiveness and new starts, which ritually aims to generate harmony in society.<sup id="cite_ref-htor_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-htor-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Regional_names.2C_rituals_and_celebrations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_names,_rituals_and_celebrations">Regional names, rituals and celebrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Regional names, rituals and celebrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Holi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hindi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a>: <span lang="hi">होली</span>, <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi</a>: <span lang="mr">होळी</span>, <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a>: <span lang="ne">होली</span>, <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa">ਹੋਲੀ</span>, <a href="/wiki/Kannada_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kannada language">Kannada</a>: <span lang="kn">ಹೋಳಿ</span>, <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telugu</a>: <span lang="te">హోళి</span>) is also known as <i><b>Phakuwa</b></i> or <i><b>Phagwah</b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a>: <span lang="as">ফাকুৱা</span>), <b>Festival of Colours</b>, or <i><b>Dola jātra</b></i> <b>(<a href="/wiki/Odia_language" title="Odia language">Odia</a>: <span lang="or">ଦୋଳଯାତ୍ରା</span>)</b> in <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, and as <i><b>Dol Jatra</b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a>: <span lang="as">দ’ল যাত্ৰা</span>) or <i><b>Basanto utsav</b></i> ("spring festival") in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>. The customs and celebrations vary between regions of India.
</p><p>Holi is of particular significance in the Braj region, which includes locations traditionally associated with the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Krishna" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Krishna">Lord Krishna</a>: <a href="/wiki/Mathura,_Uttar_Pradesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathura, Uttar Pradesh">Mathura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vrindavan" title="Vrindavan">Vrindavan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nandgaon,_Uttar_Pradesh" title="Nandgaon, Uttar Pradesh">Nandgaon, Uttar Pradesh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Barsana" title="Barsana">Barsana</a>, which become touristic during the season of Holi.<sup id="cite_ref-ind_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ind-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside India and Nepal, Holi is observed by the minority Hindus in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> as well in countries with large Indian subcontinent <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a> populations such as <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>. The Holi rituals and customs outside South Asia also vary with local adaptations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Holi%22">"Holi"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Holi%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Holi%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Holi%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Holi%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Holi%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Assam">Assam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Assam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holi, also called Phakuwa/Doul (ফাকুৱা/দৌল) in <a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a>, is celebrated all over <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>. Locally called Doul Jatra, associated with Satras of <a href="/wiki/Barpeta" title="Barpeta">Barpeta</a>, Holi is celebrated over two days. On the first day, the burning of clay huts are seen in Barpeta and lower Assam which signifies the legends of Holika. On the second day of it, Holi is celebrated with colour powders. The Holi songs in chorus devoted to Lord Krishna are also sung in the regions of Barpeta.
</p>
<h4><span id="Bihar.2FJharkhand"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bihar/Jharkhand">Bihar/Jharkhand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bihar/Jharkhand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holi is known as <i>Phaguwa</i> in the local <a href="/wiki/Bhojpuri" class="mw-redirect" title="Bhojpuri">Bhojpuri</a> dialect. In this region as well, the legend of Holika is prevalent. On the eve of Phalgun Poornima, people light bonfires. They put dried cow dung cakes, wood of the Araad or Redi tree and Holika tree, grains from the fresh harvest and unwanted wood leaves in the bonfire. At the time of Holika people assemble near the pyre. The eldest member of the gathering or a <a href="/wiki/Purohit" title="Purohit">purohit</a> initiates the lighting. He then smears others with colour as a mark of greeting. Next day the festival is celebrated with colours and a lot of frolic. Traditionally, people also clean their houses to mark the festival.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Holi Milan is also observed in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, where family members and well-wishers visit each other's family, apply colours (<a href="/wiki/Abir" class="mw-redirect" title="Abir">abeer</a>) on each other's faces, and on feet, if elderly. Usually this takes place on the evening of Holi day after Holi with wet colours is played in the morning through the afternoon. Due to large-scale internal migration issues faced by the people, recently this tradition has slowly begun to transform, and it is common to have Holi Milan on an entirely different day either before or after the actual day of Holi.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Children and youths take extreme delight in the festival. Though the festival is usually celebrated with colours, in some places people also enjoy celebrating Holi with water solutions of mud or clay. Folk songs are sung at high pitch and people dance to the sound of the <a href="/wiki/Dholak" title="Dholak">dholak</a> (a two-headed hand-drum) and the spirit of Holi. Intoxicating <a href="/wiki/Bhang" title="Bhang">bhang</a>, made from <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>, milk and spices, is consumed with a variety of mouth-watering delicacies, such as <a href="/wiki/Pakora" title="Pakora">pakoras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thandai" title="Thandai">thandai</a>, to enhance the mood of the festival.<sup id="cite_ref-HuffPostBhang_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HuffPostBhang-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Goa">Goa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Goa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shigmo" title="Shigmo">Shigmo</a></div>
<p>Holi is locally called Ukkuli in <a href="/wiki/Konkani_language" title="Konkani language">Konkani</a>. It is celebrated around the Konkani temple called Gosripuram temple. It is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goan</a> or Konkani spring <a href="/wiki/Festival" title="Festival">festival</a> known as <b>Śigmo</b> or शिगमो in <a href="/wiki/Konkani_language" title="Konkani language">Koṅkaṇī</a> or <i>Śiśirotsava</i>, which lasts for about a month. The colour festival or Holi is a part of longer, more extensive spring festival celebrations.<sup id="cite_ref-gsg_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gsg-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Holi festivities (but not Śigmo festivities) include: <i>Holika Puja</i> and <i>Dahan</i>, <i>Dhulvad</i> or <i>Dhuli vandan</i>, <i>Haldune</i> or offering yellow and saffron colour or <i>Gulal</i> to the deity.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gujarat">Gujarat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gujarat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_(1).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Holi_%281%29.JPG/170px-Holi_%281%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/Holi_%281%29.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="227" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_(1).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Celebration of Spring by Krishna and Radha", 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Miniature_(illuminated_manuscript)" title="Miniature (illuminated manuscript)">miniature</a>; in the <a href="/wiki/Guimet_Museum" title="Guimet Museum">Guimet Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, Holi is a two-day festival. On the evening of the first-day people light the bonfire. People offer raw coconut and corn to the fire. The second day is the festival of colour or "Dhuleti", celebrated by sprinkling coloured water and applying colours to each other. <a href="/wiki/Dwarka" title="Dwarka">Dwarka</a>, a coastal city of Gujarat, celebrates Holi at the Dwarkadheesh temple and with citywide comedy and music festivities.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Falling in the Hindu month of <i>Phalguna</i>, Holi marks the agricultural season of the <a href="/wiki/Rabi_crop" title="Rabi crop">rabi crop</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ahmedabad" title="Ahmedabad">Ahmedabad</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, in western India, a pot of buttermilk is hung high over the streets and young boys try to reach it and break it by making human pyramids. The girls try to stop them by throwing coloured water on them to commemorate the pranks of Krishna and the cowherd boys to steal butter and "<a href="/wiki/Gopi" title="Gopi">gopis</a>" while trying to stop the girls. The boy who finally manages to break the pot is crowned the Holi King. Afterwards, the men, who are now very colourful, go out in a large procession to "alert" people of Krishna's possible appearance to steal butter from their homes.
</p><p>In some places there is a custom in undivided Hindu families that the woman beats her brother-in-law with a sari rolled up into a rope in a mock rage and tries to drench him with colours, and in turn, the brother-in-law brings sweets (Indian desserts) to her in the evening.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Jammu_.26_Kashmir"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jammu_&amp;_Kashmir">Jammu &amp; Kashmir</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Jammu &amp; Kashmir">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Jammu_%26_Kashmir" class="mw-redirect" title="Jammu &amp; Kashmir">Jammu &amp; Kashmir</a>, Holi celebrations are much in line with the general definition of Holi celebrations: a high-spirited festival to mark the beginning of the harvesting of the summer crop, with the throwing of coloured water and powder and singing and dancing.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Karnataka">Karnataka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Karnataka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Traditionally, in rural <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a> children collect money and wood in the weeks prior to Holi, and on "Kamadahana" night all the wood is put together and lit. The festival is celebrated for two days. People in northern parts of Karnataka prepare special food on this day.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Sirsi,_Karnataka" title="Sirsi, Karnataka">Sirsi</a>, Karnataka, Holi is celebrated with a unique folk dance called "Bedara Vesha", which is performed during the nights beginning five days before the actual festival day. The festival is celebrated every alternate year in the town, which attracts a large number of tourists from different parts of India.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Maharashtra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a>, Holi Purnima is also celebrated as Shimga, festivities that last five to seven days. A week before the festival, youngsters go around the community, collecting firewood and money. On the day of Shimga, the firewood is heaped into a huge pile in each neighborhood. In the evening, the fire is lit. Every household brings a meal and dessert, in the honour of the fire god. Puran Poli is the main delicacy and children shout "Holi re Holi puranachi poli". Shimga celebrates the elimination of all evil. The colour celebrations here take place on the day of <a href="/wiki/Rang_Panchami" title="Rang Panchami">Rang Panchami</a>, five days after Shimga. During this festival, people are supposed to forget and forgive any rivalries and start new healthy relations with all.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Manipur">Manipur</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Manipur">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Manipuris" class="mw-redirect" title="Manipuris">Manipuris</a> celebrate Holi for 6 days. Here, this holiday merges with the festival of <a href="/wiki/Yaosang" title="Yaosang">Yaosang</a>. Traditionally, the festival commences with the burning of a thatched hut of hay and twigs. Young children go from house to house to collect money, locally known as <i>nakadeng</i> (or <i>nakatheng</i>), as gifts on the first two days. The youths at night perform a group folk dance called <a href="/wiki/Thabal_chongba" title="Thabal chongba">Thabal chongba</a> on the full moon night of Lamta (<a href="/wiki/Phalgun" class="mw-redirect" title="Phalgun">Phalgun</a>), traditionally accompanied by folk songs and rhythmic beats of the indigenous drum, but nowadays by modern bands and <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a>. In Krishna temples, devotees sing devotional songs, perform dances and celebrate with <i>aber</i> (<i>gulal</i>) wearing traditional white and yellow turbans. On the last day of the festival, large processions are taken out to the main Krishna temple near <a href="/wiki/Imphal" title="Imphal">Imphal</a> where several cultural activities are held. In recent decades, <a href="/wiki/Yaosang" title="Yaosang">Yaosang</a>, a type of Indian sport, has become common in many places of the valley, where people of all ages come out to participate in a number of sports that are somewhat altered for the holiday.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Odisha">Odisha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Odisha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg/150px-Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg/225px-Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg/300px-Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elevation_of_the_black_stone_arch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1822 drawing showing elevation of a black stone arch in <a href="/wiki/Puri" title="Puri">Puri</a>, Odisha. It carried <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavite</a> gods and goddess, the ritual noted to be a part of the Holi festival.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The people of <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a> celebrate "Dola" on the day of Holi where the icons of <a href="/wiki/Jagannath" title="Jagannath">Jagannath</a> replace the icons of Krishna and Radha. Dola Melana, processions of the deities are celebrated in villages and <a href="/wiki/Bhog" title="Bhog">bhoga</a> is offered to the deities. "Dola yatra" was prevalent even before 1560 much before Holi was started where the idols of <a href="/wiki/Jagannath" title="Jagannath">Jagannath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balabhadra" title="Balabhadra">Balabhadra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subhadra" title="Subhadra">Subhadra</a> used to be taken to the "Dolamandapa" (podium in <a href="/wiki/Jagannath_temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Jagannath temple">Jagannath temple</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ray2007_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ray2007-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> People used to offer natural colours known as "abira" to the deities and apply on each other's feats.<sup id="cite_ref-Pati2001_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pati2001-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Punjab">Punjab</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Punjab">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>, the eight days preceding Holi are known as luhatak.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Sekhon (2000) states that people start throwing colours  many days before Holi.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holi is preceded by <a href="/wiki/Holika_Dahan" title="Holika Dahan">Holika Dahan</a> the night before when a fire is lit. Historically, the Lubana community of Punjab celebrated holi "with great pomp and show. The Lubanas buried a pice and betel nut. They heaped up cow-dung cakes over the spot and made a large fire. When the fire had burnt out, they proceeded to hunt for the pice and betel-nut. Whosoever found these, was considered very lucky."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in Punjab, Holi was also associated with making fools of others. Bose writing in Cultural Anthropology: And Other Essays in 1929 noted that "the custom of playing Holi-fools is prevalent in Punjab".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the day of Holi, people engage in throwing colours<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> on each other.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> For locals, Holi marks the end of winter. The Punjabi saying <i>Phaggan phal laggan</i> (<i>Phagun</i> is the month for fructifying) exemplifies the seasonal aspect of Holi. Trees and plants start blossoming from the day of Basant and start bearing fruit by Holi.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During Holi in <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>, walls and courtyards of rural houses are enhanced with drawings and paintings similar to <a href="/wiki/Rangoli" title="Rangoli">rangoli</a> in South India, <i>mandana</i> in Rajasthan, and rural arts in other parts of India. This art is known as <i>chowk-poorana</i> or <i>chowkpurana</i> in Punjab and is given shape by the peasant women of the state. In courtyards, this art is drawn on cloth. The art includes drawing tree motifs, flowers, ferns, creepers, plants, peacocks, palanquins, geometric patterns along with vertical, horizontal and oblique lines. These arts add to the festive atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Folk theatrical performances known as swang or nautanki take place during Holi,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> with the latter originating in the Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> According to Self (1993), Holi fairs are held in the Punjab which may go on for many days.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Bose (1961) states that "in some parts of Punjab, Holi is celebrated with wrestling matches".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Telangana">Telangana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Telangana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As in other parts of India, in rural <a href="/wiki/Telangana" title="Telangana">Telangana</a>, children celebrate <i>kamuda</i> and collect money, rice, corn and wood for weeks prior to Holi, and on Kamudha night all the wood is put together and set on fire.
</p><p>Hindus celebrate Holi as it relates to the legend of Kama Deva. Holi is known by three names: Kamavilas, Kaman Pandigai and Kama-Dahanam<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uttar_Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Uttar Pradesh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lath_mar_Holi" class="mw-redirect" title="Lath mar Holi">Lath mar Holi</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:48.5px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Samaj_gathering_during_Lathmar_hoil.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Samaj_gathering_during_Lathmar_hoil.jpg/200px-Samaj_gathering_during_Lathmar_hoil.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Samaj_gathering_during_Lathmar_hoil.jpg/300px-Samaj_gathering_during_Lathmar_hoil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Samaj_gathering_during_Lathmar_hoil.jpg/400px-Samaj_gathering_during_Lathmar_hoil.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2784" data-file-height="1856" /></a></div></div>

</p>
