		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:53px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lath_Mar_Holi_at_Braj.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lath_Mar_Holi_at_Braj.jpg/200px-Lath_Mar_Holi_at_Braj.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="124" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lath_Mar_Holi_at_Braj.jpg/300px-Lath_Mar_Holi_at_Braj.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lath_Mar_Holi_at_Braj.jpg/400px-Lath_Mar_Holi_at_Braj.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="280" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Braj" title="Braj">Braj</a> region of North India, women have the option to playfully hit men who save themselves with shields; for the day, men are culturally expected to accept whatever women dish out to them. This ritual is called <a href="/wiki/Lath_mar_Holi" class="mw-redirect" title="Lath mar Holi"><i>Lath Mar</i> Holi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:48.5px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_play_of_colors_then_a_dance_at_Holi_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/A_play_of_colors_then_a_dance_at_Holi_India.jpg/200px-A_play_of_colors_then_a_dance_at_Holi_India.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/A_play_of_colors_then_a_dance_at_Holi_India.jpg/300px-A_play_of_colors_then_a_dance_at_Holi_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/A_play_of_colors_then_a_dance_at_Holi_India.jpg/400px-A_play_of_colors_then_a_dance_at_Holi_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A play of colours then a dance at a Hindu temple near Mathura, at Holi.
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Barsana" title="Barsana">Barsana</a>, a town near <a href="/wiki/Mathura,_Uttar_Pradesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathura, Uttar Pradesh">Mathura</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Braj" title="Braj">Braj</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, celebrates <a href="/wiki/Lath_mar_Holi" class="mw-redirect" title="Lath mar Holi">Lath mar Holi</a> in the sprawling compound of the Radha Rani temple. Thousands gather to witness the Lath Mar Holi when women beat up men with sticks as those on the sidelines become hysterical, sing Holi songs and shout "Sri Radhey" or "Sri Krishna".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The Holi songs of Braj mandal are sung in pure Braj, the local language. Holi celebrated at <a href="/wiki/Barsana" title="Barsana">Barsana</a> is unique in the sense that here women chase men away with sticks. Males also sing provocative songs in a bid to invite the attention of women. Women then go on the offensive and use long staves called <a href="/wiki/Fighting_stick" class="mw-redirect" title="Fighting stick">lathis</a> to beat the men, who protect themselves with shields.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mathura, in the Braj region, is the birthplace of Lord <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Vrindavan" title="Vrindavan">Vrindavan</a> this day is celebrated with special <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a> and the traditional custom of worshipping Lord Krishna; here the festival lasts for sixteen days.<sup id="cite_ref-ind_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ind-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> All over the Braj region <sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and neighboring places like <a href="/wiki/Hathras" title="Hathras">Hathras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aligarh" title="Aligarh">Aligarh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a>, Holi is celebrated in more or less the same way as in Mathura, <a href="/wiki/Vrindavan" title="Vrindavan">Vrindavan</a> and Barsana.
</p><p>A traditional celebration includes <i>Matki Phod</i>, similar to <i><a href="/wiki/Dahi_Handi" title="Dahi Handi">Dahi Handi</a></i> in Maharashtra and Gujarat during <a href="/wiki/Krishna_Janmashtami" title="Krishna Janmashtami">Krishna Janmashtami</a>, both in the memory of god Krishna who is also called <i>makhan chor</i> (literally, butter thief). This is a historic tradition of the Braj region as well as the western region of India.<sup id="cite_ref-Gellner2009_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gellner2009-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> An earthen pot filled with butter or other milk products is hung high by a rope. Groups of boys and men climb on each other's shoulders to form pyramids to reach and break it, while girls and women sing songs and throw coloured water on the pyramid to distract them and make their job harder.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> This ritual sport continues in Hindu diaspora communities.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside Braj, in the <a href="/wiki/Kanpur" title="Kanpur">Kanpur</a> area, Holi lasts seven days with colour. On the last day, a grand fair called <i>Ganga Mela</i> or the <i>Holi Mela</i> is celebrated. This <i>Mela</i> (<a href="/wiki/Fair" title="Fair">fair</a>) was started by freedom fighters who fought British rule in the <a href="/wiki/First_Indian_War_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="First Indian War of Independence">First Indian War of Independence</a> in 1857 under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Nana_Saheb" title="Nana Saheb">Nana Saheb</a>. The <i>Mela</i> is held at various <a href="/wiki/Ghats" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghats">ghats</a> along the banks of the River <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganga</a> in Kanpur, to celebrate the Hindus and Muslims who together resisted the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> forces in the city in 1857. On the eve of Ganga Mela, all government offices, shops, and courts generally remain closed. The Ganga Mela marks the official end of "The Festival of Colours" or Holi in <a href="/wiki/Kanpur" title="Kanpur">Kanpur</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Gorakhpur" title="Gorakhpur">Gorakhpur</a>, the northeast district of Uttar Pradesh, the day of Holi starts with a special <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a>. This day, called "Holi Milan", is considered to be the most colourful day of the year, promoting brotherhood among the people.  People visit every house and sing Holi songs and express their gratitude by applying coloured powder (<a href="/wiki/Abir" class="mw-redirect" title="Abir">Abeer</a>). It is also considered the beginning of the year, as it occurs on the first day of the Hindu calendar year (Panchang).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Uttarakhand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kumauni_Holi" title="Kumauni Holi">Kumauni Holi</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kumaoni_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kumaoni people">Kumaoni</a> Holi in <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a> includes a musical affair. It takes different forms such as the Baithki Holi, the Khari Holi and the Mahila Holi. In Baithki Holi and Khari Holi, people sing songs with a touch of melody, fun, and spiritualism. These songs are essentially based on classical <a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">ragas</a>. Baithki Holi (बैठकी होली), also known as <i>Nirvan Ki Holi</i>, begins from the premises of temples, where <i>Holiyars</i> (होल्यार) sing Holi songs and people gather to participate, along with playing classical music. The songs are sung in a particular sequence depending on the time of day; for instance, at noon the songs are based on Peelu, Bhimpalasi and Sarang ragas, while evening songs are based on the ragas such as Kalyan, Shyamkalyan and Yaman. The <b>Khari Holi</b> (खड़ी होली) is mostly celebrated in the rural areas of Kumaon. The songs of the Khari Holi are sung by the people, who, sporting traditional white <i><a href="/wiki/Churidar" title="Churidar">churidar payajama</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kurta" title="Kurta">kurta</a></i>, dance in groups to the tune of ethnic musical instruments such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Dhol" title="Dhol">dhol</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hurka" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurka">hurka</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Kumaon region, the Holika pyre, known as <i>Cheer</i> (चीर), is ceremonially built in a ceremony known as <i>Cheer Bandhan</i> (चीर बंधन) fifteen days before Dulhendi. The <i>Cheer</i> is a bonfire with a green <i>Paiya</i> tree branch in the middle. The <i>Cheer</i> of every village and neighborhood is rigorously guarded as rival <i><a href="/wiki/Mohalla" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohalla">mohallas</a></i> try to playfully steal each other's <i>cheer</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The colours used on Holi are derived from natural sources. Dulhendi, known as <i>Charadi</i> (छरड़ी) (from <i>Chharad</i> (छरड़)), is made from flower extracts, ash and water. Holi is celebrated with great gusto much in the same way all across North India.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Bengal">West Bengal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: West Bengal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, Holi is known by the name of "Dol Jatra", "Dol Purnima" or the "Swing Festival". The festival is celebrated in a dignified manner by placing the icons of Krishna and <a href="/wiki/Radha" title="Radha">Radha</a> on a picturesquely decorated <a href="/wiki/Palanquin" class="mw-redirect" title="Palanquin">palanquin</a> which is then taken round the main streets of the city or the village. On the <a href="/wiki/Dol_Purnima" title="Dol Purnima">Dol Purnima</a> day in the early morning, students dress up in saffron-coloured or pure white clothes and wear garlands of fragrant <a href="/wiki/Flowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Flowers">flowers</a>. They sing and dance to the accompaniment of musical instruments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ektara" title="Ektara">ektara</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dubri&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dubri (page does not exist)">dubri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">Veena</a>. The devotees take turns to swing them while women dance around the swing and sing devotional songs. During these activities, the men keep spraying coloured water and coloured powder, <a href="/wiki/Abir" class="mw-redirect" title="Abir">abir</a>, at them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nepal">Nepal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Nepal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holika_Dahan,_Kathamandu,_Nepal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Holika_Dahan%2C_Kathamandu%2C_Nepal.jpg/220px-Holika_Dahan%2C_Kathamandu%2C_Nepal.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Holika_Dahan%2C_Kathamandu%2C_Nepal.jpg/330px-Holika_Dahan%2C_Kathamandu%2C_Nepal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Holika_Dahan%2C_Kathamandu%2C_Nepal.jpg/440px-Holika_Dahan%2C_Kathamandu%2C_Nepal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holika_Dahan,_Kathamandu,_Nepal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Preparing for Holika Dahan, Kathamandu, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG/220px-Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG/330px-Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG/440px-Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_in_Nepal_2016.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Locals Celebrating Holi In <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a>, Nepal</div></div></div>
<p>Holi, along with many other Hindu festivals, is celebrated in Nepal as a national festival. It is an important major Nepal-wide festival along with <a href="/wiki/Dashain" title="Dashain">Dashain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tihar_(festival)" title="Tihar (festival)">Tihar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dipawali" class="mw-redirect" title="Dipawali">Dipawali</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-northey80_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northey80-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> It is celebrated in the Nepali month of Phagun (same date as Indian Holi), and signifies the legends of the Hindu god Krishna.<sup id="cite_ref-northey80_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northey80-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Newar Buddhists and others worship Saraswati shrine in Vajrayogini temples and 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg/220px-Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg/330px-Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg/440px-Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Two_women_celebrating_Holi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two women celebrating Holi in Kathmandu, Nepal</div></div></div>
<p>celebrate the festival with their Hindu friends.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Traditional concerts are held in most cities in Nepal, including <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narayangarh,_Chitwan" class="mw-redirect" title="Narayangarh, Chitwan">Narayangarh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pokhara" title="Pokhara">Pokhara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Itahari" title="Itahari">Itahari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hetauda" title="Hetauda">Hetauda</a>,  and <a href="/wiki/Dharan,_Nepal" class="mw-redirect" title="Dharan, Nepal">Dharan</a>, and are broadcast on television with various celebrity guests.
</p><p>People walk through their neighbourhoods to celebrate Holi by exchanging colours and spraying coloured water on one another. A popular activity is the throwing of water balloons at one another, sometimes called <i>lola</i> (meaning water balloon).<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Many people mix <i><a href="/wiki/Bhang" title="Bhang">bhang</a></i> in their drinks and food, as is also done during <a href="/wiki/Shivaratri" class="mw-redirect" title="Shivaratri">Shivaratri</a>. It is believed that the combination of different colours at this festival takes all sorrow away and makes life itself more colourful.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_diaspora">Indian diaspora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Indian diaspora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg/220px-Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg/330px-Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg/440px-Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_Festival_Of_Colorus_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Holi Festival Of Colours London</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG/220px-Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG/330px-Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG/440px-Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richmond_Hill_Holi_2013_Drummers.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drummers of Indo-Caribbean community celebrating <i>Phagwah</i> (Holi) in New York City, 2013</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors,_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors%2C_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg/220px-A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors%2C_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors%2C_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg/330px-A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors%2C_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors%2C_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg/440px-A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors%2C_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2200" data-file-height="1462" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_celebration_of_Holi_Festival_of_Colors,_Utah_United_States_2013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A celebration of Holi Festival in the United States</div></div></div>
<p>Over the years, Holi has become an important festival in many regions wherever <a href="/wiki/Non-resident_Indian_and_person_of_Indian_origin" title="Non-resident Indian and person of Indian origin">Indian diaspora</a> were either taken as <a href="/wiki/Indian_indenture_system" title="Indian indenture system">indentured labourers</a> during <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">colonial era</a>, or where they emigrated on their own, and are now present in large numbers such as in Africa, North America, Europe, Latin America, and parts of Asia such as Fiji.<sup id="cite_ref-online.wsj.com_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-online.wsj.com-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holi_Festival_of_Colours_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holi_Festival_of_Colours-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suriname">Suriname</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Suriname">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holi is a national holiday in <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>. It is called <i>Phagwa</i> festival, and is celebrated to mark the beginning of spring and Hindu mythology. In Suriname, Holi Phagwa is a festival of colour. It is customary to wear old white clothes on this day, be prepared to get them dirty and join in the colour throwing excitement and party.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinidad_and_Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Trinidad and Tobago">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Phagwa is normally celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a> on the Sunday closest to the actual date of Phagwah. It is celebrated with a lot of colour and splendour, along with the singing on traditional Phagwah songs or <a href="/wiki/Chowtal" title="Chowtal">Chowtal</a> (gana).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Guyana">Guyana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Guyana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Phagwah is a national holiday in <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, and peoples of all races and religions participate in the celebrations.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The main celebration in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Guyana" title="Georgetown, Guyana">Georgetown</a> is held at the Mandir in Prashad Nagar.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiji">Fiji</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Fiji">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Indo-Fijians celebrate Holi as festival of colours, folksongs, and dances. The folksongs sung in <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a> during Holi season are called <i>phaag gaaian</i>. Phagan, also written as Phalgan, is the last month of the Hindu calendar. Holi is celebrated at the end of Phagan. Holi marks the advent of spring and ripening of crops in Northern India. Not only it is a season of romance and excitement, folk songs and dances, it is also an occasion of playing with powder, perfumes, and colours. Many of the Holi songs in Fiji are around the theme of love-relationship between Radha and Krishna.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mauritius">Mauritius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Mauritius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holi in <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> comes close on the heels of Shivaratri. It celebrates the beginning of spring, commemorating good harvests and the fertile land. Hindus believe it is a time of enjoying spring’s abundant colours and saying farewell to winter. It is considered one of the most exhilarating religious holidays in existence. During this event, participants hold a bonfire, throw coloured powder at each other, and celebrate wildly.<sup id="cite_ref-Holi_Festival_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holi_Festival-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holi is celebrated in many US states. It is usually hosted in temples or cultural halls. Members of Hindu associations and volunteers assist in hosting the event along with temple devotees. Some of the places known to celebrate Holi are <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick,_New_Jersey" title="New Brunswick, New Jersey">New Brunswick</a> (NJ), <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Fork,_Utah" title="Spanish Fork, Utah">Spanish Fork</a> (Utah), <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a> (TX), <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a> (TX), <a href="/wiki/South_El_Monte,_California" title="South El Monte, California">South El Monte</a> (CA), <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> (MA), <a href="/wiki/Potomac,_Maryland" title="Potomac, Maryland">Potomac</a> (MD), and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> (IL).<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Indonesia, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Indonesians" title="Indian Indonesians">Indian Indonesians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu peoples</a> celebrate Holi as festival of colours. The main celebrations in Medan and Bali.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Holi is celebrated by the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_Hindus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani Hindus">minority Hindu</a> population in Pakistan. Community events by Hindus have been reported by Pakistani media in various cities such as <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hazara,_Pakistan" title="Hazara, Pakistan">Hazara</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rawalpindi" title="Rawalpindi">Rawalpindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyderabad,_Sindh" title="Hyderabad, Sindh">Hyderabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holi was not a public holiday in Pakistan from 1947 to 2016. Holi along with Diwali for Hindus, and Easter for Christians, was adopted as public holiday resolution by Pakistan's parliament in 2016, giving the local governments and public institutions the right to declare Holi as a holiday and grant leave for its minority communities, for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> This decision has been controversial, with some Pakistanis welcoming the decision, while others criticising it, with the concern that declaring Holi a public holiday advertises a Hindu festival to Pakistani children.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Traditional_Holi"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Holi_colours">Holi colours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Holi colours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhak_(Butea_monosperma)_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Dhak_%28Butea_monosperma%29_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg/220px-Dhak_%28Butea_monosperma%29_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Dhak_%28Butea_monosperma%29_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg/330px-Dhak_%28Butea_monosperma%29_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Dhak_%28Butea_monosperma%29_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg/440px-Dhak_%28Butea_monosperma%29_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhak_(Butea_monosperma)_flowers_in_Kolkata_W_IMG_4219.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flowers of Dhak or <a href="/wiki/Palash" class="mw-redirect" title="Palash">Palash</a> are used to make traditional colours</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_sources_of_colours">Traditional sources of colours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Traditional sources of colours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The spring season, during which the weather changes, is believed to cause viral fever and cold. The playful throwing of natural coloured powders, called <a href="/wiki/Gulal_(Holi_festival)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gulal (Holi festival)">gulal</a> has a medicinal significance: the colours are traditionally made of <a href="/wiki/Neem" class="mw-redirect" title="Neem">Neem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kumkum" class="mw-redirect" title="Kumkum">Kumkum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turmeric" title="Turmeric">Haldi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bilva" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilva">Bilva</a>, and other medicinal herbs prescribed by <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Āyurvedic</a> doctors.
</p><p>Many colours are obtained by mixing primary colours. Artisans produce and sell many of the colours from natural sources in dry powder form, in weeks and months preceding Holi. Some of the traditional natural plant-based sources of colours are:<sup id="cite_ref-keholi_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keholi-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orange_and_red">Orange and red</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Orange and red">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The flowers of <a href="/wiki/Butea_monosperma" title="Butea monosperma">palash</a> or tesu tree, also called the flame of the forest, are typical source of bright red and deep orange colours. Powdered fragrant red sandal wood, dried hibiscus flowers, madder tree, radish, and pomegranate are alternate sources and shades of red. Mixing lime with turmeric powder creates an alternate source of orange powder, as does boiling saffron (kesar) in water.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Green">Green</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Green">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mehndi" title="Mehndi">Mehendi</a> and dried leaves of gulmohur tree offer a source of green colour. In some areas, the leaves of spring crops and herbs have been used as a source of green pigment.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yellow">Yellow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Yellow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_shop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Holi_shop.jpg/220px-Holi_shop.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Holi_shop.jpg/330px-Holi_shop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Holi_shop.jpg/440px-Holi_shop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_shop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colours for Holi on sale at a market in <a href="/wiki/Mysore" title="Mysore">Mysore</a></div></div></div>
<p><i>Haldi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Turmeric" title="Turmeric">turmeric</a>) powder is the typical source of yellow colour. Sometimes this is mixed with chickpeas, gram or other flour to get the right shade. Bael fruit, amaltas, species of chrysanthemums, and species of marigold are alternate sources of yellow.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue">Blue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Blue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigofera" title="Indigofera">Indigo plant</a>, Indian berries, species of grapes, blue hibiscus and <a href="/wiki/Jacaranda" title="Jacaranda">jacaranda</a> flowers are traditional sources of blue colour for Holi.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magenta_and_purple">Magenta and purple</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Magenta and purple">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Beetroot" title="Beetroot">Beetroot</a> is the traditional source of magenta and purple colour. Often these are directly boiled in water to prepare coloured water.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brown">Brown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Brown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Dried <a href="/wiki/Tea#Preparation" title="Tea">tea</a> leaves offer a source of brown coloured water. Certain clays are alternate source of brown.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Black">Black</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Black">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Species of grapes, fruits of <a href="/wiki/Phyllanthus_emblica" title="Phyllanthus emblica">amla</a> (gooseberry) and vegetable carbon (charcoal) offer gray to black colours.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthetic_colours">Synthetic colours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Synthetic colours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural colours were used in the past to celebrate Holi safely by applying <a href="/wiki/Turmeric" title="Turmeric">turmeric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandalwood" title="Sandalwood">sandalwood</a> paste, extracts of flowers and leaves. As the spring-blossoming trees that once supplied the colours used to celebrate Holi have become rarer, chemically produced industrial dyes have been used to take their place in almost all of urban India. Due to the commercial availability of attractive pigments, slowly the natural colours are replaced by synthetic colours. As a result, it has caused mild to severe symptoms of skin irritation and inflammation. Lack of control over the quality and content of these colours is a problem, as they are frequently sold by vendors who do not know their origin
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Holi_powder">Holi powder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Holi powder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_Impact">Health Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Health Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

style="padding:1px">Holi in Basantpur, Durbar Square</div></td></tr></tbody></table><div>The festival of Holi is massively popular in India</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A 2007 study found that <a href="/wiki/Malachite_green" title="Malachite green">malachite green</a>, a synthetic bluish-green dye used in some colours during Holi festival, was responsible for severe eye irritation in Delhi, if eyes were not washed upon exposure. Though the study found that the pigment did not penetrate through the cornea, malachite green is of concern and needs further study.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another 2009 study reports that some colours produced and sold in India contain metal-based industrial dyes, causing an increase in skin problems to some people in the days following Holi. These colours are produced in India, particularly by small informal businesses, without any quality checks and are sold freely in the market. The colours are sold without labeling, and the consumer lacks information about the source of the colours, their contents, and possible toxic effects. In recent years, several nongovernmental organisations have started campaigning for safe practices related to the use of colours. Some are producing and marketing ranges of safer colours derived from natural sources such
</p><p>as vegetables and flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These reports have galvanised a number of groups into promoting more natural celebrations of Holi. Development Alternatives, Delhi and Kalpavriksh,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> – Kalpavriksh Environment Action Group, Pune, The CLEAN India campaign<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and Society for Child Development, through its Avacayam Cooperative Campaign<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> have launched campaigns to help children learn to make their own colours for Holi from safer, natural ingredients. Meanwhile, some commercial companies such as the National Botanical Research Institute have begun to market "herbal" dyes, though these are substantially more expensive than the dangerous alternatives. However, it may be noted that many parts of rural India have always resorted to natural colours (and other parts of festivities more than colours) due to availability.
</p><p>In urban areas, some people wear nose mask and sunglasses to avoid inhaling pigments and to prevent chemical exposure to eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An alleged environmental issue related to the celebration of Holi is the traditional Holika bonfire, which is believed to contribute to deforestation. Activists estimate Holika causes 30,000 bonfires every year, with each one burning approximately 100 kilograms (220.46 lbs) of wood.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> This represents less than 0.0001% of 350 million tons of wood India consumes every year, as one of the traditional fuels for cooking and other uses.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of heavy metal-based pigments during Holi is also reported to cause temporary <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> pollution, with the water systems recovering to pre-festival levels within 5 days.
</p><p><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flammability">Flammability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Flammability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 2015, hundreds of concert-goers in <a href="/wiki/Bali_District" title="Bali District">Bali District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> were severely injured in the <a href="/wiki/Formosa_Fun_Coast_explosion" class="mw-redirect" title="Formosa Fun Coast explosion">Formosa Fun Coast explosion</a>, including fifteen who died later in hospital,<sup id="cite_ref-Victim_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Victim-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> after three tons of corn starch powder mixed with food colouring was sprayed onto the crowd at a high velocity, causing a massive explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The method of powder application at the concert created "an extremely dense dust cloud over the stage and its immediate vicinity".<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> People near the stage were standing ankle deep in coloured corn starch powder and the powder was suspended into the air using air blowers as well as compressed gas canisters.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Initial investigations into the explosion showed the ignition of the suspended corn starch powder was likely caused by a cigarette or spark. An Asia One report<sup id="cite_ref-DeadlyPowderBBC_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeadlyPowderBBC-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> states that such an explosion can occur, under certain conditions, not just with corn starch but with powder form of any agricultural product such as "powdered milk, soya flour, cornflour, rice dust, spice powders, sugar, tapioca, cocoa powder, coconut shell dust, coffee dust, garlic powder, grass dust, malted hops, lemon peel dust, oat flour, peanut skins, tea and tobacco", and that "the crucial element is not the composition of the powder itself, but whether it's deployed under high pressure with a flame nearby."<sup id="cite_ref-DeadlyPowderBBC_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeadlyPowderBBC-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Williamson, flammable powder or dust suspended in air in high concentrations is <a href="/wiki/Dust_explosion" title="Dust explosion">explosive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DeadlyPowderBBC_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeadlyPowderBBC-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Williamson notes that "dust cloud explosions can only occur if the dust concentration is within certain limits. In general the lowest concentration of dust that can give a dust explosion is around 50–100 g/m<sup>3</sup> (1–3 oz/cu yd) and the maximum is 2–3&#160;kg/m<sup>3</sup> (3–5&#160;lb/cu&#160;yd). These limits are dependent on the particular chemical in question. It is usually easy to see if a cloud is explosible, as visibility through a dust cloud — even at the lowest concentrations — is impaired."<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During traditional Holi celebrations in India, Rinehart writes, colours are exchanged in person by "tenderly applying coloured powder to another person's cheek", or by spraying and dousing others with buckets of coloured water.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_other_cultures">Influence on other cultures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Influence on other cultures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:130px;height:95px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Holi_Festival_2017_in_Italy.01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Holi style="padding:1px"></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div>The festival of Holi is increasingly celebrated in many parts of the world outside India</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Holi is celebrated as a social event in parts of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Festival_of_Colors:_Holi_NYC_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Festival_of_Colors:_Holi_NYC-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> For example, at <a href="/wiki/Sri_Sri_Radha_Krishna_Temple_(Spanish_Fork)" title="Sri Sri Radha Krishna Temple (Spanish Fork)">Sri Sri Radha Krishna Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Fork,_Utah" title="Spanish Fork, Utah">Spanish Fork, Utah</a>, <i>NYC Holi Hai</i> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a><sup id="cite_ref-NYC_Holi_Hai_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYC_Holi_Hai-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Festival of Colors: Holi NYC</i> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Festival_of_Colors:_Holi_NYC_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Festival_of_Colors:_Holi_NYC-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Holi is celebrated as the Festival of Color, where thousands of people gather from all over the United States, play and mingle.<sup id="cite_ref-wpnonhindus_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wpnonhindus-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Festival_of_Colors:_Holi_NYC_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Festival_of_Colors:_Holi_NYC-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holi-inspired_events">Holi-inspired events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Holi-inspired events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A number of Holi-inspired social events have also surfaced, particularly in Europe and the United States, often organised by companies as for-profit or charity events with paid admission, and with varying scheduling that does not coincide with the actual Holi festival. These have included Holi-inspired <a href="/wiki/Music_festival" title="Music festival">music festivals</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Festival_Of_Colours_Tour" title="Festival Of Colours Tour">Festival Of Colours Tour</a> and Holi One<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> (which feature timed throws of Holi powder), and <a href="/wiki/5K_run" title="5K run">5K run</a> franchises such as <a href="/wiki/The_Color_Run" title="The Color Run">The Color Run</a>, Holi Run and Color Me Rad,<sup id="cite_ref-sanjosecom-colormerad_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanjosecom-colormerad-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> in which participants are doused with the powder at per-kilometre checkpoints.<sup id="cite_ref-colorrun-aljazeera_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colorrun-aljazeera-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj-holifight_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-holifight-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BiH_Color_Festival" title="BiH Color Festival">BiH Color Festival</a> is a Holi-inspired <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music_festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic music festival">electronic music festival</a> held annually in <a href="/wiki/Br%C4%8Dko" title="Brčko">Brčko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There have been concerns that these events <a href="/wiki/Cultural_appropriation" title="Cultural appropriation">appropriate</a> and trivialise aspects of Holi for commercial gain—downplaying or completely ignoring the cultural and spiritual roots of the celebration.<sup id="cite_ref-colorrun-aljazeera_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colorrun-aljazeera-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj-holifight_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-holifight-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Organisers of these events have argued that the costs are to cover various key aspects of their events, such as safe colour powders, safety and security, and entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj-holifight_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-holifight-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-holicalendars-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-holicalendars_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-holicalendars_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Since ancient times, the Indian subcontinent has had several major <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu calendars</a>, which places Holi and other festivals on different local months even though they mean the same date. Some Hindu calendars emphasise the solar cycle, some the lunar cycle. Further, the regional calendars feature two traditions of Amanta and Purnimanta systems, wherein the similar sounding months refer to different parts of a lunar cycle, thus further diversifying the nomenclature. The Hindu festival of Holi falls on the first (full moon) day of <i>Chaitra</i> lunar month's dark fortnight in the Purnimanta system, while the same exact day for Holi is expressed in Amanta system as the lunar day of <i>Phalguna Purnima</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fuller292_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fuller292-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Both time measuring and dating systems are equivalent ways of meaning the same thing, they continue to be in use in different regions.<sup id="cite_ref-fuller292_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fuller292-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In regions where the local calendar places it in its <i>Phalguna</i> month, Holi is also called <i>Phaguwa</i>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-wpnonhindus-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-wpnonhindus_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-wpnonhindus_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Lyford, Chris (5 April 2013). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/local/hindu-spring-festivals-increase-in-popularity-and-welcome-non-hindus/2013/04/05/d4828e00-9bf4-11e2-9a79-eb5280c81c63_story.html">"Hindu spring festivals increase in popularity and welcome non-Hindus"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>. New York City<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 February</span> 2016</span>. <q>Despite what some call the reinvention of Holi, the simple fact that the festival has transcended cultures and brings people together is enough of a reason to embrace the change, others say. In fact, it seems to be in line with many of the teachings behind Holi festivals.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Washington+Post&amp;rft.atitle=Hindu+spring+festivals+increase+in+popularity+and+welcome+non-Hindus&amp;rft.date=2013-04-05&amp;rft.aulast=Lyford&amp;rft.aufirst=Chris&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.washingtonpost.com%2Flocal%2Fhindu-spring-festivals-increase-in-popularity-and-welcome-non-hindus%2F2013%2F04%2F05%2Fd4828e00-9bf4-11e2-9a79-eb5280c81c63_story.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHoli" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://m.vam.ac.uk/collections/item/O403895/elevation-of-the-black-stone-drawing-unknown/?q">"Elevation of the black stone arch"</a>. <i>V&amp;A: Search the Collections</i>. Victoria and Albert Museum<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 April</span> 2016</span>. <q>Object history note: The arch is covered with figures of Vaishnavite gods and hung with rings. A crowd of Hindus are celebrating the festival of the Dol Jatra or Swing festival in which the image of Vishnu and his consort are swung in a throne suspended by chains from the rings of the arch. The celebration is part of the Holi festival and takes place at the full moon of the month of Phalguna (February to March).</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=V%26A%3A+Search+the+Collections&amp;rft.atitle=Elevation+of+the+black+stone+arch&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fm.vam.ac.uk%2Fcollections%2Fitem%2FO403895%2Felevation-of-the-black-stone-drawing-unknown%2F%3Fq&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHoli" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
